id="MJXp-Span-148" style="vertical-align: -1.012em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-149">16</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-150">c</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-151"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-152" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-153"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-154" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-155"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-156" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-157">Fe</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-158" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-159">3</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-160">+</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-161">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-162">2</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-163">+</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-164" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-165" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-166">16</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-167">d</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-168"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-169" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-170">O</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-171" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-172">4</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Fe</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>3</mn><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mrow><mn>8</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Fe</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>3</mn><mo>+</mo><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mn>16</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msub><mover><mo linebreak="nobreak">→</mo><mrow><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="italic">x</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi></mrow></msub><mi mathvariant="normal">Li</mi></mrow></mover><msub><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msubsup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Li</mi></mrow><mrow><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="italic">x</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mo>+</mo></mrow></msubsup><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Fe</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>3</mn><mo>+</mo><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mn>16</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">c</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msup><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Fe</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>3</mn><mo>+</mo><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn><mo>+</mo></mrow></msup><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mn>16</mn><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi></mrow></msub><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">O</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>4</mn></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Beyond a certain fraction of Li per formula unit of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, the insertion process becomes nonviable; a breakage of the original crystal system occurs, and conversion takes place.</div><div class="NLM_p">In addition to the attractiveness of the intercalation to conversion model of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> itself is an attractive anode material. It is cheap, possesses a conductivity of σ = 2 × 10<sup>4</sup> Sm<sup>–1</sup> (just an order shy of metallic conductivity), and leaves an almost nonexistent environmental footprint. Thus, the Thackeray model engendered significant research on Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as a viable anode for LIBs and SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14 ref15'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 13 to 15">(13−15)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">He et al. and Zhang et al. performed exhaustive studies of the Thackeray model on lithiation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanomaterials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> Through <i>ex situ</i> and <i>in situ</i> experimental techniques, they demonstrated the validity of the model proposed by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a> in the first cycle. The reversible capacity was, however, close to two electron equivalents or ∼200 mA h/g. Similar efforts in demonstrating that the model of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a> works for SIBs have, however, shown mixed success. While many of these studies started out with the Thackeray model as the motivation of their research, they ended up focusing on improving electrochemical performance by optimizing the extraneous features of materials and the process conditions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11" aria-label="reference 9 to 11">(9−11)</a> As we show later in the <a class="ref internalNav" href="#sec3" aria-label="Results and Discussion">Results and Discussion</a> section, Komaba et al.’s assertion that Na<sup>+</sup> ions are inserted into the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> inverse spinel crystal structure is contradicted by their own data. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Our research is thus motivated by the lack of mechanistic understanding of conversion-type electrochemistry in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as an anode in SIBs. We focus on the fact that the commonly accepted notion that the science developed for LIBs can be applicable to SIBs has been disproved but has continued to be practiced. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16" aria-label="reference 16">(16)</a> We investigated Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage behaviors in bulk and NP forms of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. While our gravimetric capacity for the anode was commensurate with what other research groups have observed, it is still about one tenth of the theoretical capacity. For a molecular-level understanding of electrochemical Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as functions of depth of discharge (DoD) and state of charge (SoC), electrochemical analysis, post-mortem X-ray-based and spectroscopic characterization, and X-ray emission spectroscopy were employed. These studies reveal that the intercalation to conversion mechanism proposed by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a> is unworkable for Na<sup>+</sup> ions in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. In addition, our attempts at performing a density functional theory calculation of the stability of the Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> system (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>) yielded unphysical results, pointing to the inability of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> system to accommodate Na<sup>+</sup> ions. Finally, we provide several possible explanations for the observed electrochemical behaviors of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anode in SIBs based on a combination of solid electrolyte interphase formation/evolution, surface chemistry of NPs, and ionic storage and conductivity enhancement at nanointerfaces (nanoionics).</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec2_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i6" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i6"> Material Synthesis and Characterization</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Monodisperse magnetite Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs were synthesized via reductive decomposition of Fe-precursors in a high-boiling-point organic solvent as described in ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref17'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref17" aria-label="reference 17">(17)</a>. For some studies, the inert surfactants were removed by annealing the NPs in an oven at 300 °C in the ambient for 1 h. The size and the crystallinity of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs were characterized using an FEI F20 transmission electron microscope (TEM) and Bruker Instruments X-ray diffractometer (XRD). The morphology of the electrode slurry was investigated with a TESCAN MIRA field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM). Sodiation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrodes as functions of DoD and SoC was qualitatively studied through energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS)-assisted elemental mapping, utilizing the same TESCAN MIRA FESEM system. To determine the inherent electrochemical properties of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> [iron (II, III) oxide] was procured from Sigma-Aldrich [nanopowder, 50–100 nm particle size (SEM), 97% trace metals basis].</div></div><div id="sec2_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7"> Electrode and Cell Fabrication</h3><div class="NLM_p last">Slurries were made with 70 wt % Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP active materials, 20 wt % carbon black (C45, TIMCAL), and 10 wt % polyvinylidene fluoride (PVdF, Solvay) binder. 2–3 mL of <i>N</i>-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP, Sigma-Aldrich) was added, and the mixture was mixed in a slurry mixer. Films of the slurry were fabricated by tape casting on a carbon-coated copper current collector. Electrochemical coin-type cells were fabricated using these Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> slurry films as the working electrodes and punched sodium foil disks as the counter/reference electrodes. Typical mass loading of the active material Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs on the electrode (15.5 mm diameter) was in the range 1.5–2.5 mg. 1 M sodium hexafluorophosphate (NaPF<sub>6</sub>, Sigma-Aldrich) in 50:50 vol % of ethylene carbonate (EC, Tomiyama Pure Chemical Industries) and propylene carbonate (PC, Tomiyama Pure Chemical Industries) with a water content of &lt;30 ppm was used as an electrolyte. A Whatmann GF/F glass fiber was used as a separator. For comparison, similar studies on the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> slurry films were performed using Li-ion half-cells with a 1.0 M lithium hexafluorophosphate (LiPF<sub>6</sub>, Sigma-Aldrich) in a 50:50 vol % mixture of EC and diethyl carbonate (DEC, Sigma-Aldrich) electrolyte and a Celgard 2400 polypropylene separator.</div></div><div id="sec2_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8"> Electrochemical Analysis and Diagnostics</h3><div class="NLM_p last">The cells were cycled galvanostatically at a rate of approximately C/20, which was 50 mA/g for the conversion reaction of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> with Na<sup>+</sup> ions (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>). Most cells were cycled for 100 cycles. The primary focus of this research was, however, to determine the crystal structure evolution of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as functions of DoD and SoC. Accordingly, a series of cells were fabricated with electrodes based on Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs annealed to remove surfactants prior to slurry formation and subjected to fractional galvanostatic discharge/charge cycles. The voltage vs specific capacity profiles of these cells labeled A–H considering their discharge and charge states (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>) are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. Upon arrival at the desired DoD and SoC, the cells were removed from the testing station. Each cell was then disassembled and the electrode retrieved. Following this, the electrodes were gently cleaned by using dimethyl carbonate (DMC, Sigma-Aldrich) for further characterization.</div></div><div id="sec2_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i9" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i9"> Advanced Characterizations</h3><div class="NLM_p">XRD patterns and Raman scattering spectra were collected from the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP anodes as functions of DoD and SoC (samples A–H in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). Raman spectra were excited using the linearly polarized output of a solid state laser (785 nm) with 7 mW of power in a spot diameter of ∼1.0 μm at the sample and were dispersed and detected using a single-axis monochromator equipped with a charge-coupled detector array (Horiba XploRA, Edison, NJ). All Raman spectra were collected unpolarized and in the backscattering geometry.</div><div class="NLM_p last">X-ray emission spectroscopy (XES) studies were carried out on the cycled anodes (samples A–H) to determine the occupation of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and the possible reaction pathway from the initial intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into the inverse spinel structure of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to the eventual conversion to metal NPs and sodium oxides (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>). Specifically, the cation configurations in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> were characterized using XES for reliable information on cation oxidation states. This was accomplished by a site selective XES analysis by taking advantage of the spin, oxidation state, and ligand-sensitive Kβ<sub>1,3</sub> + Kβ<sub>2,5</sub> spectra. The XES spectra were collected using five spherically bent analyzer crystals [Si(533) for Fe Kβ] arranged in a Rowland geometry and detected using a Pilatus 100K area detector. The energy of the incident X-ray beam was tuned by means of a Si(111) double-crystal monochromator and was set to 9 keV. The beam size used was 100 × 100 μm. A Rh-coated mirror was used for harmonic rejection. To minimize the attenuation of the fluorescent radiation, the area between the spherical analyzers, detector, and sample was filled with helium gas. The emission spectra were collected with a step size of 0.30 eV. For all samples, up to five scans each were carried out at fixed position on the sample and were averaged to obtain the final spectra for the sample.</div></div><div id="sec2_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i10" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i10"> Density Functional Theory (DFT)-Based Calculations</h3><div class="NLM_p last">DFT calculations were performed using Quantum Espresso software, which utilizes a plane wave basis set in the expansion of the Kohn–Sham single particle wave functions. The ion–electron interactions were approximated using the projector augmented wave (PAW) potentials. Both the kinetic energy cutoff and Γ-centered Monkhorst–Pack <i>k</i>-point grid were tested for convergence against a 1 meV/atom threshold. It was determined that a 700 eV kinetic energy cutoff and a 12 × 12 × 12/1 × 1 × 1 <i>k</i>-point grid for the unit cell were sufficient. All structural relaxations and energy calculations were performed using the conjugate-gradient algorithm, and the ground state configuration was considered reached upon net forces on atoms falling below 0.01 eV/Å. Visualization for Electronic and Structural Analysis (VESTA) software was used to build the crystal structures.</div></div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i11">Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12"> Materials</h3><div class="NLM_p">Electrochemical sodiation was carried out on bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (average crystallite size 50 nm) and Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs. The average diameter of the as-synthesized Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs was approximately 8.93 nm, as determined by averaging based on several TEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a). A high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b) and selected area diffraction patterns (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c) demonstrate that the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs are crystalline, and the lattice fringes are indexed to the (311) planes. XRD (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d) and the accompanied crystal plane designations show that the as-synthesized Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs crystallize in the inverse spinel structure. Using the most intense peak at 2θ = 35.262°, we determined a lattice constant of 8.564 Å, which is very similar to what is available in the literature. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9 ref10 ref11 ref12" aria-label="reference 9 to 12">(9−12)</a> In addition, an average crystallite size of 8.72 nm was determined using the Scherrer equation with the fwhm of the (311) peak, which is close to the value based on TEM measurements. FESEM images (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>e) taken from the electrodes show that the carbon flakes are well mixed with the NPs on copper substrates.</div><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/medium/am2c13016_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Synthesized Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs were characterized using TEM (a), HRTEM (b), selective area diffraction (c), and XRD (d). (e) FE-SEM images taken from the pristine electrodes show good mixing of the different components of the electrode slurry. The scale bars in panels a, b, and e are 5, 2, and 500 nm, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c13016&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13"> Electrochemical Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p">The theoretical capacity of the conversion-type Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anode is 926 mA h/g in SIBs (and LIBs), as determined from <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq1" aria-label="eq 1">eq 1</a>, compared with the practical capacity of around 327 mA h/g for hard carbon. Li half-cells with Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP electrodes were tested first to ensure whether our Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs are well synthesized and electrochemically active. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, the synthesized Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based anode in Li half-cells demonstrates an initial capacity of around 1250 mA h/g and subsequent reversible capacity of over 500 mA h/g; these capacities are on par with or larger than what was observed in recent articles. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13 ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> In addition to the galvanostatic discharge/charge cycling, the electrochemical behaviors of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based anodes in Li-ion half cells were analyzed using a corresponding differential capacity (d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i>) plot (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3c</a>). We note that the first cycle discharge curve in the voltage vs gravimetric capacity profile (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3a</a>) clearly shows all of the sloping features and the plateaus that were seen in refs  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref13'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13" aria-label="reference 13">(13)</a> and <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref14'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14" aria-label="reference 14">(14)</a>, which describe the systematic intercalation to conversion reactions of the Thackeray’s model and confirm that our materials synthesis and battery fabrication methodology are all sound.</div><div class="NLM_p">Na half-cells, cycled galvanostatically at a rate of approximately C/20, show much lower capacities for both bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-based electrodes and synthesized Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrodes compared with those of Li half-cells. The reversible capacity of the Na half-cells with bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-based anodes is around 54 mA h/g with an initial 160 mA h/g capacity, while the cells with Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based anodes show a reversible capacity of 94 mA h/g─almost a 100% increase compared to bulk─with an initial 401 mA h/g capacity as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,b,d. It is noteworthy that the reversible capacity is further improved by another 10% with an initial 539 mA h/g capacity when the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs are processed to remove the organic ligands from their surfaces, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c,d. The detailed electrochemical analysis of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based anodes in Na-ion half cells based on a differential capacity (d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i>) plot is noted in the Supporting Information (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4</a>).</div><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/medium/am2c13016_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Voltage vs gravimetric capacity profiles of bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>- (a), synthesized Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP- (b), and combusted Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based anodes (c). (d) Cycling performance of those electrodes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c13016&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Before proceeding into the next part of the paper, we note here that this paper is not about improving the electrochemical performance of sodium-ion half-cells with Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-based anodes. This has been done in previous studies, and our results either exceed or are competitive with the currently published results. For example, in ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a>, the first cycle discharge capacity is in the range of 150 mA h/g, and in ref  <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref11'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref11" aria-label="reference 11">(11)</a>, it is 643 mA h/g. In addition, composite active materials composed of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and a second complementary material like FeS, Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>, graphene, carbon nanoparticle, and multi-heteroatom-dopants exhibits capacities in the range 169–350 mA h/g. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref18 ref19 ref20 ref21 ref22" aria-label="reference 18 to 22">(18−22)</a> Other anode materials like MoS<sub>2</sub>-reduced graphene oxides, NiS<sub>1.03</sub> nanoparticles with S-doped reduced graphene oxide, and 3D nanoflower-like V<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> exhibit sodium storage capacities as high as 572 mA h/g at a 100 mA/g rate. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26" aria-label="reference 23 to 26">(23−26)</a> Our research is instead focused on providing a fundamental and mechanistic understanding of the ion storage behaviors and possible failure mechanisms in single phase Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as a conversion-type anode in SIBs.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i14" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i14"> Phase Transitions</h3><div class="NLM_p">Structural changes of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anodes as functions DoD and SoC were investigated utilizing XRD and Raman spectroscopy. Based on the XRD patterns of pristine Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>d), and of the pristine and cycled electrodes (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a), it is clear that the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs are crystallized in the inverse spinel structure. X-ray peaks corresponding to Cu metal and Fe and Al impurities present in the Cu current collector are also seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a. It is notable that there are negligible changes in the intensity and position of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> peaks as the electrodes are discharged and charged at different DoD and SoC, which is not too different from earlier experimental and simulation studies by Komaba et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> indicating that the (220) peak would weaken upon discharge if migration of Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions from the tetrahedral 8a sites to the octahedral 16c sites were to take place. When they performed electrochemical insertion studies of Li<sup>+</sup> ions into nanocrystalline Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, they found that the (220) reflection peak indeed weakened upon discharge and regained strength after charge, indicating the validity of the model proposed by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>. However, their electrochemical insertion studies of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into nanocrystalline Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> show negligible changes in the (220) peak intensity and position, similar to our results in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b. For clarity, we also show the most intense peak (311) (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c), which also shows insignificant changes as functions of DoD and SoC. Therefore, it can be concluded that electrochemical insertion and deinsertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into/from the inverse spinel structure of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> could be unworkable, correlating the observed low capacity of the material (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>).</div><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/medium/am2c13016_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) XRD patterns of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP anodes as functions of DoD and SoC, along with that of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs and of the pristine electrode. A comparison of the cycled electrodes with the original NPs shows no discernible changes as the electrodes undergo systematic discharging and charging. Closer looks at the diffraction peaks corresponding to the reflection from the (220) (b) and (311) (c) crystal planes show negligible changes. The color designations for panel c vis-à-vis DoD and SoC are identical to those of panel b.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c13016&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">Complementary Raman analyses were performed on the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based slurry and electrodes as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>. Nanoscale unique properties in materials including quantum confinement, surface strain, and chemistry are extremely important for battery performance, and these can be studied extensively utilizing Raman spectroscopy. Due to the potential of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as a battery anode, a vast amount of literature on Raman studies of the material already exists. Specifically, Shebanova et al. showed that it is difficult to detect all five Raman active modes─symmetries of A<sub>1g</sub>, E<sub>g</sub>, T<sub>2g</sub>, T<sub>2g</sub>, and T<sub>2g</sub>─of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and the Raman modes across different studies vary significantly. For example, the difference in Raman peak positions of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> was found to be as much as 30 cm<sup>–1</sup> when the exciting laser source was changed from 532 to 785 nm. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27 ref28'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27 ref28" aria-label="reference 27 and 28">(27,28)</a> Therefore, the Raman spectra of synthesized Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs in the slurry and in the electrodes with conductive carbons and binders were carefully assigned the following normal mode symmetries based on an extensive literature survey: T<sub>2g</sub> (1) at 226.4 cm<sup>–1</sup>, E<sub>g</sub> at 292.8 cm<sup>–1</sup>, T<sub>2g</sub> (2) at 411.9 cm<sup>–1</sup>, T<sub>2g</sub> (3) at 492.4 cm<sup>–1</sup>, and A<sub>1g</sub> at 608.7 cm<sup>–1</sup>, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a. In addition, characteristic Raman shifts known as the G and D bands from the conductive carbons in the electrode were also assigned. Then, Raman spectra were collected from the pristine and cycled electrodes at different DoD and SoC (samples A–H, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>). The Raman spectrum of the pristine electrode is identical to that of the mixed slurry indicating that the electrode coating and drying steps may not affect the material’s structure. More remarkable is that there are negligible changes in the Raman spectra from the subsequent samples. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, we plotted the individual modes as functions of DoD and SoC and see almost perfect overlap of all the Raman shifts of individual symmetries from each sample.</div><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/medium/am2c13016_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. Raman spectra of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP anodes as functions of DoD and SoC, along with that of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based slurry and of the pristine electrode as references. (a) Full range scans show the five Raman active modes as well as the G and D bands of carbon. All Raman spectra were peak-fitted to determine the peak locations. (b) Individual Raman modes as functions of DoD and SoC show negligible changes. The left inset of panel b shows a constant value for the ratio of the T<sub>2g</sub> (2) peak intensity that is responsible for the vibration of the 8a Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions to the A<sub>g</sub> intensity. The right inset shows the normal mode vibration of the T<sub>2g</sub> (2) mode.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c13016&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p last">Normal mode enumeration shows that the Raman active modes of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> result from collective motions of the Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions located at the 8a Wyckoff positions (<i>T</i><sub><i>d</i></sub> symmetry) and the O atoms at the 32e Wyckoff positions (<i>O</i><sub><i>h</i></sub> symmetry). The Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> cations located at the 16c Wyckoff positions do not participate in Raman modes. Specifically, the 8a Fe<sup>3+</sup> mode is T<sub>2g</sub> (2) at 411.9 cm<sup>–1</sup>. If the displacement of the Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions from the 8a Wyckoff positions were to take place according to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>, gradual weakening of this Fe<sup>3+</sup> mode intensity, compared to the other modes, could be observed. Furthermore, if the Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions were to partially vacate the Raman active 8a positions, as prescribed in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>, we should observe changes to the existing mode frequency due to change in mode normal mass. The frequency Γ of a normal mode is inversely proportional to the square root of the reduced mass <i>M</i><sub>Γ</sub> of the atoms participating in the mode: <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-173"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-174">Γ</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-175">∞</span><span class="MJXp-msqrt" id="MJXp-Span-176"><span class="MJXp-surd"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale5" style="font-size: 318%; margin-top: 0.044em; margin-left: -0.273em;">√</span></span><span class="MJXp-root"><span class="MJXp-rule" style="border-top: 0.113em solid;"></span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-177"><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-178" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box MJXp-script"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-179"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-180">1</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box MJXp-script"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-181"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-182"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-183" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-184">M</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-185" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-186">r</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-187" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-msqrt" id="MJXp-Span-188"><span class="MJXp-surd"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale5" style="font-size: 318%; margin-top: 0.044em; margin-left: -0.273em;">√</span></span><span class="MJXp-root"><span class="MJXp-rule" style="border-top: 0.113em solid;"></span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-189"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-190" style="margin-left: 0.111em; margin-right: 0.167em;">∑</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-191"><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-192" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box MJXp-script"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-193"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-194">1</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box MJXp-script"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-195"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-196">i</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-197"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-198" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-199">M</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-200" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-201">i</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="inline"><mi mathvariant="normal">Γ</mi><mi>∞</mi><msqrt><mrow><mfrac><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>M</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">r</mi></mrow></msub></mrow></mfrac></mrow></msqrt><mo>=</mo><msqrt><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">∑</mo><mrow><mfrac><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi><msub><mrow><mi>M</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">i</mi></mrow></msub></mrow></mfrac></mrow></mrow></msqrt></math></script>. As shown in the right inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, only two tetrahedral Fe<sup>3+</sup> ions participate in the T<sub>2g</sub> (2) vibration, and as such, migration of a small fraction of these ions to the 16c position would change the frequency drastically. Furthermore, we have plotted the ratios of the T<sub>2g</sub> (2) mode intensity to the A<sub>1g</sub> mode intensity in the left inset of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, and the ratios show an almost constant value. The facts that the frequency of the T<sub>2g</sub> (2) modes does not change and that the intensity of the T<sub>2g</sub> (2) mode with respect to the A<sub>1g</sub> mode does not change as functions of DoD and SoC point to our assertion that Na<sup>+</sup> ions may not be incorporated into the inverse spinel structure in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, further pointing to the validity of our assertion based on the XRD studies (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>).</div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i16" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i16"> Transition Metal Redox Chemistry</h3><div class="NLM_p">Following the observation of nonviability of Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion into the crystal structure of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, we turned to more direct study to observe the redox couple Fe<sup>3+</sup>/Fe<sup>2+</sup> in the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> system by analyzing the cycled Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anodes using X-ray emission spectroscopy (XES). XES studies commence with creating a core hole in transition metals (e.g., Fe) using X-ray beams. The core hole created after the ionization of an electron is unstable and decays rapidly via fluorescence (or Auger) processes. The fluorescence events following the photoabsorption of X-ray photons can be detected using XES. The most probable fluorescence event is a 2p electron refilling the 1s core hole, giving rise to a so-called Kα emission line. Approximately 1 order of magnitude less likely is a 3p to 1s transition that creates a Kβ emission line. For a transition metal with unpaired d electrons, the 3p–3d exchange coupling results in spectral splitting, giving rise to Kβ<sub>1,3</sub> and Kβ′ spectral features. Additionally, one can also observe the least probable event─a valence electron refilling the 1s core hole. This gives rise to valence-to-core (VtC) emission lines, Kβ<sub>2,5</sub> and Kβ″, which correspond to transitions from ligand <i>n</i>p and <i>n</i>s orbitals to the metal 1s, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29 ref30 ref31'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 29 to 31">(29−31)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">The relevance of XES studies to our investigation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as the SIB anode is that Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> have different Kβ<sub>1,3</sub> and Kβ′ spectral features in principle, and Na<sup>+</sup> insertion into the crystal structure of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> should change some of the Fe<sup>3+</sup> to Fe<sup>2+</sup> to satisfy charge neutrality. As a result, we should observe a systematic change of the XES features as functions of DoD and SoC.</div><div class="NLM_p">The resolution between the XES spectra of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> is, however, more nuanced, due to the “accidental” coincidence of d orbital filling, covalency, and spin ordering in Fe. In general, the splitting between Kβ<sub>1,3</sub> and Kβ′ decreases, and the position of Kβ<sub>1,3</sub> shifts to lower energy as the oxidation state of the metal increases. In other words, removing d-electrons results in less unpaired electrons, less excited state multiplets, and less ways for 3p–3d exchange coupling. For the redox couple Fe<sup>2+</sup>(d<sup>6</sup>)/Fe<sup>3+</sup>(d<sup>5</sup>), this simple multiplet model does not suffice. Removing an electron from Fe<sup>2+</sup> to create Fe<sup>3+</sup><i>increases</i> the number of unpaired electrons which pushes the Kβ<sub>1,3</sub> peak to the higher energy side. Removing an electron from Fe<sup>2+</sup>, however, lessens the number of ways of creating excited states with the ligands, the effect being known in the literature as configuration interaction driven by covalency. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> Configuration interaction pushes the Fe<sup>3+</sup> Kβ<sub>1,3</sub> peak to lower energy compared to Fe<sup>2+</sup>. The result is that the resolution between the Kβ XES features of Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> is not easy to detect.</div><div class="NLM_p">These limitations of XES studies of Fe<sup>2+</sup>/Fe<sup>3+</sup> are, however, on individual Fe<sup>2+</sup> and Fe<sup>3+</sup> systems. The crystalline environment of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is different. In the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> crystal system, the competing influence of spin vs covalency should not be so severe as to wash out the resolution because, in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> system, the Fe centers are always coordinated by only oxide ligands, and the geometry is constrained by the lattice; thus, there’s less room for geometric changes that could complicate the analysis. We thus expect to see resolvable resolution in our studies. Specifically, in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, half of the Fe<sup>3+</sup> cations fill the smaller tetrahedral 8a sites, and the second half of the Fe<sup>3+</sup> cations and the Fe<sup>2+</sup> cations occupy the larger 16d octahedral sites. As shown in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>, the incorporation of one Li<sup>+</sup> ion removes the tetrahedral Fe<sup>3+</sup> to octahedral positions resulting in both Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup> being in the same ligand environment. Fe<sup>3+</sup> and Fe<sup>2+</sup>, being in different spin configuration but the same ligand environment, should demonstrate the effect of spin only, and as such, we should be able to see systematic changes in XES feature as the anode undergoes systematic discharging and recharging.</div><div class="NLM_p">The XES spectra from the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP anodes are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>, and we see no such effects in the XES scans from the samples. The XES results correlate with the XRD and Raman analysis dictating that the model proposed by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a> is not valid for SIBs. A further higher-resolution investigation was carried out on samples A–H (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>) to determine possible small changes in the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> crystal as a result of sodiation. In the high-energy resolution fluorescence detected (HERFD) X-ray studies, one scans through the X-ray absorption energy and detects at a single emission energy. By combining XES with X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) in a resonant measurement, one can further enhance the selectivity. HERFD scan results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>c. Again, no distinction is seen even in these scans, pointing to our initial assumption that Na<sup>+</sup> ions are not inserted into the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> structure.</div><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/medium/am2c13016_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. XES spectra of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based anodes as functions of DoD and SoC along with that of a pristine electrode as a reference. Kβ<sub>1,3</sub> and Kβ′ features (a) and Kβ<sub>2,5</sub> and Kβ″ features (b) show negligible change as functions of DoD and SoC. (c) Higher-resolution HERFD scans also show negligible changes.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c13016&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure></div><div id="sec3_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i17" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i17"> Structural Relaxation Calculations of Na, Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub></h3><div class="NLM_p">DFT calculations were performed using Quantum Espresso software to study the structural relaxations (including lattice vectors) of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, body centered cubic (bcc) Na, and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> using the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) approximations. Following <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>, one Na atom is inserted into the 16c Wyckoff position of the 14-atom Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> inverse spinel unit cell, as shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1b</a>. The voltage for the intercalation of a Na<sup>+</sup> ion into Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is found as the difference in total energies of the different constituents as <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-202"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-203"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-204"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-205"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-206">V</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-207"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-208" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-209"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-210"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-211" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-212" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-213"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-214"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-215" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-216">E</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-217" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-218">intercalated</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-219" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">−</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-220"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-221" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-222">E</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-223" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-224">empty</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-225" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">−</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-226"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-227" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-228">E</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-229" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-230">metal</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-231"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-232">n</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-233" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">)</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-4"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>V</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>intercalated</mi></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>empty</mi></mrow></msub><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>metal</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Here, <i>E</i><sub>intercalated</sub> is the energy of the intercalated anode (e.g., Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>); <i>E</i><sub>empty</sub> is the energy of the empty anode (e.g., Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>); <i>E</i><sub>metal</sub> is the energy per atom of a pure metal anode; and <i>n</i> is the valency of the intercalated cation. We note here that these calculations are based solely on the internal energy of each constituent. The more rigorous Gibbs free energy would require consideration of volume and entropic effects, but it has been shown to result in small corrections to internal energy while significantly increasing the computational time. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32" aria-label="reference 32">(32)</a></div><div class="NLM_p last">Structural relaxations of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.5) resulted in no discernible changes of the initial crystal structures. The volume of the unit cell, however, increased by 7% with <i>x</i> = 0.5. Calculations of voltages using <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq3" aria-label="eq 3">eq 3</a>, however, resulted in negative voltage (<i>V</i> = −0.35 V vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>), meaning that sodiation of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in the form of Na<sub><i>x</i></sub>Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (<i>x</i> = 0.5) is unfavorable. Recent publications on DFT calculations of the intercalation of Al<sub>0.20</sub> in α- and ζ-V<sub>2</sub>O<sub>5</sub> showed negative voltage values similar to ours: disadvantages of Al incorporation in the vanadium oxide cathode are well documented. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33 ref34 ref35" aria-label="reference 32 to 35">(32−35)</a> Hence, our DFT investigation points to the impracticality of the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions into Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and further validates our experimental findings.</div></div><div id="sec3_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i19" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i19"> Surface Chemistry Analysis and Electrochemical Diagnostics</h3><div class="NLM_p">Having discussed the nonviability of Na<sup>+</sup> ion insertion to conversion reactions in the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> anode (<a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>) based on electrochemical analysis and advanced characterization results, we shift our attention to other possible mechanisms for the low capacity observed in our experiments and previous publications. SEM analysis and EDS elemental mapping were performed on the samples as functions of DoD and SoC to verify whether the origin of the capacity observed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a> could be Na<sup>+</sup> adsorption/desorption onto the active electrode. As seen in the EDS plots and elemental mapping in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S6 and S7</a>, Na is uniformly distributed in the discharged electrodes. Considering the ratio of the EDS intensities of Na to Fe a measure of the amount of Na<sup>+</sup> ions adsorbed onto the electrodes, we plotted the ratio as functions of DoD and SoC in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a. While the analysis is qualitative, <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figures S6 and S7</a> and <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a do indicate that the Na<sup>+</sup> ions are adsorbed on the electrodes progressively with increased DoD and desorbed from the electrodes gradually with increased SoC. We note here that six EDS elemental maps at each DoD and SoC were averaged to arrive at the plot of the ratios in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a. That this analysis is indeed qualitative is apparent in the last data point of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>a that shows increased Na content in sample H compared to that in sample G.</div><figure id="fig6" data-index="6" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 6</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/medium/am2c13016_0006.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0006.jpeg" id="gr6" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 6. (a) The ratio of the EDS intensities of Na to Fe as functions of DoD and SoC qualitatively indicates that Na<sup>+</sup> ions are adsorbed on the electrodes progressively with increased DoD and desorbed gradually with increased SoC. In panel a, the <i>x</i>-axis refers to the cycled electrodes A–H, with P being the pristine electrode. (b) Possible Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage and conductivity enhancement in the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrode could entail an attractive interaction between the Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the electrolyte and the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs in the electrode that results in a narrow interfacial region devoid of Na<sup>+</sup> ions immediately outside the interface. For oxide NPs in the electrolyte, a conformal space charge region (c, orange annulus) devoid of cations is created. (d) In an ensemble of NPs in the electrolyte, both cation storage and ionic conductivity are enhanced when the conformal space charge regions reach the percolation threshold. (e) Synergistic effects between an ion conducting interface and an electron conducting electrode may enhance the charge storage capacity of electrochemical cells.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/asset/images/large/am2c13016_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.2c13016&amp;id=fig6"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>, we observe 50 mA h/g of specific capacity for bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>-based electrodes, which improves to around 100 mA h/g for Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrodes. Possible explanations for the observation could be as follows:<table class="listgroup" border="0" width="95%" list-type="label"><tbody><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(a)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">The 50 mA h/g capacity of bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and the 100 mA h/g capacity of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs could result from the formation of a solid electrolyte interphase (SEI) on the electrode surface with possible side reactions (e.g., electrolyte decomposition). As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>d, the reversible capacities upon cycling may infer the efficiency of the conductive SEI layer for Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage. In general, SEI formation on the anode side is considered a result of reductive decomposition of electrolyte. Therefore, discharge capacity is more related to SEI formation. As bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> has a bigger crystallite size than synthesized NPs, its surface area is lower, and as such, the demonstrated initial lower discharge capacity of bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as compared to that of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>NPs points to the role played by the SEI in these half cells. Further study of the formed and evolved SEI layers could shed more light on this.</p></td></tr><tr class="li1"><td valign="top">(b)</td><td colspan="5" valign="top" class="paragraph"><p class="last">Surface ion storage (e.g., mostly adsorption and desorption) on NPs is a well-known phenomenon. TEM investigations show that most NPs are endowed with surface facets, since they are nanosize versions of the original crystal (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>b). Many of these facets contain defects and can store free ions. As shown in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>, the total surface area of particles is proportional to 1/<i>r</i>, with <i>r</i> being the radius of the particles. As such, it makes qualitative sense that the capacity of electrodes composed of NPs is larger than that with bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. Quantitatively, using 100 mA h/g of capacity with 8.5 nm Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs, we calculate a storage of 3736 Na<sup>+</sup> ions on the surface of each Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP (see the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>). However, based on our previous work on Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs, we deem this number of ions per NP to be unrealistic; we have demonstrated that individual NP facets are capable of storing at best one free ion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a></p></td></tr></tbody></table></div><div class="NLM_p last">To complement the trivial size effect, we turn to a nanostructuring induced enhancement of charge storage and ionic conductivity at the heterointerface between the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrode and the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-based electrolyte with dissimilar work function, a phenomenon formally known as <i>nanoionics</i>.</div></div><div id="sec3_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i20" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i20"> Nanoionics Effects</h3><div class="NLM_p">Nanoionics effects are the enhancement of ionic conductivity and ionic storage at heterogeneous interfaces, such as between a metal and a semiconductor, between an electrolyte and an oxide (e.g., LiI–Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> system), or, as in our case, between the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-based electrolyte and the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrode. Nanoionics phenomena are qualitatively similar to Schottky barriers that result in local migration of mobile ions within a heterogeneous interphase due to the difference in work functions of the two materials. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 to 39">(37−39)</a> A space charge layer formed at such interphases can contribute to enhanced ionic and electronic conductivity, and improved charge transfer reaction kinetics. Such effects have already been observed in LIBs with RuO<sub>2</sub> electrodes that form the Li<sub>2</sub>O/Ru composite upon reduction. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> Ru does not allow Li<sup>+</sup> ions in its crystal structure but is a good electronic conductor. On the contrary, Li<sub>2</sub>O does admit Li<sup>+</sup> ions but is a poor electronic conductor. A compensatory and synergistic effect develops at the interfacial region of the Li<sub>2</sub>O/Ru composite where up to a monolayer storage of Li<sup>+</sup> ions takes places leading to high capacity and reversibility of the battery.</div><div class="NLM_p">Two types of nanoionics interactions between a Frenkel disordered crystal M<sup>+</sup>X<sup>–</sup> (e.g., AgCl and NaPF<sub>6</sub>) and a chemically inert material A (e.g., Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>) are possible. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37 ref38 ref39'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 to 39">(37−39)</a> The first is surface stabilization of M<sup>+</sup> ions by A, where the M<sup>+</sup> cations are attracted to the interface that lowers the free energy of vacancy formation and enlarges the free energy of interstitial formation compared to the intrinsic values. This creates a layer of M<sup>+</sup> ion vacancies at the interface. In the second scenario where a repulsive interaction between A and interfacial M<sup>+</sup> is more energetically favorable, the latter are driven into the interstitial sites of M<sup>+</sup>X<sup>–</sup>. The end results are the same for both cases: an interfacial region of M<sup>+</sup> ion vacancies and possibility of extrinsic conductivity enhancement at the interface. As a specific example of nanoionics at the heterogeneous interface, Maier et al. considered the addition of fine-grained insulating Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> particles to AgCl. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 40 to 42">(40−42)</a> The defect concentration in AgCl is small, but cation mobility is significant. As the basic oxide Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> adsorbs the mobile Ag<sup>+</sup> cations, excess cation vacancies are formed in the AgCl matrix adjacent to the Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub>. This composite system can now act as a high mobility conduit with a high concentration of cation vacancies. We hypothesize that a similar interaction between the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-based electrolyte and the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrode occurs in our electrochemical cells, as shown schematically in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>b.</div><div class="NLM_p">Quantitative analysis of such a system can be performed by realizing that such an effect is related to the standard free enthalpy and mass action constant, and relating the local cation concentration to the standard free enthalpy. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 40 to 42">(40−42)</a> Similar to the p–n junctions, the concentration profile can be obtained from Poisson’s equation with imposition of constant electrochemical potential and the boundary condition that the electric field (−<i>∂φ</i>/<i>∂x</i>) vanishes in the interior. The concentration profile is found to be functions of the Gibbs free energy for vacancy (or interstitial) formation, Debye length, and temperature:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-234"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-235"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-236"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-237"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-238"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-239" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-240">C</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-241" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-242">v</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-243"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-244" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.222em;">,</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-276">λ</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-277" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.222em;">,</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-278">T</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-279" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-5"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>C</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">v</mi></mrow></msub><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>C</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">v</mi></mrow></msub><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>0</mn><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mspace width=".25em"></mspace><mi>f</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>x</mi><mo>,</mo><msub><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">v</mi></mrow></msub><msup><mrow><mi>G</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>°</mn></mrow></msup><mo>,</mo><mi>λ</mi><mo>,</mo><mi>T</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Here, <i>C</i><sub>v</sub>(0) is the concentration at the interface (<i>x</i> = 0), <i>x</i> is the position measured from the interface and going inward into M<sup>+</sup>X<sup>–</sup>, Δ<sub>v</sub><i>G</i><sup>0</sup> is the Gibbs free energy of vacancy formation in M<sup>+</sup>X<sup>–</sup>, λ is the Debye length, and <i>T</i> is the temperature. The Debye length (λ) determines the fall of the interfacial charge distribution into the interior and is given by<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-280"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-281"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-282"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-283"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-284">λ</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-285"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-286" style="margin-left: 0.333em; In addition to enhanced cation storage, cation conductivity can also drastically improve at the space charge region (λ). The global conductivity is influenced by the so-called parallel switching that takes into account the connectivity of the space charge layers (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>d), similar to the percolation scenario. Rigorous calculations for the M<sup>+</sup>X<sup>–</sup>/A system by Maier et al. show that the total conductance is given by<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq6"><div class="equation"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-351">2</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-352" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-353"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-354" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-355">β</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow
