id="MJXp-Span-364">A</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-365" style="margin-left: 0.111em; margin-right: 0.111em;">/</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-366"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-367" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-368">r</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-369" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-370">A</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-371" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span><span class="MJXp-msqrt" id="MJXp-Span-372"><span class="MJXp-surd"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale8" style="font-size: 201%; margin-top: 0.07em; margin-left: -0.109em;">√</span></span><span class="MJXp-root"><span class="MJXp-rule" style="border-top: 0.089em solid;"></span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-373"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-374" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">∈</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-375"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-376" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-377" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">∈</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-378" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-379">0</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-380">R</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-381">T</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-382" style="margin-left: 0.111em; margin-right: 0.111em;">/</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-383"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-384" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-385">V</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-386" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-387">m</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-388"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-389" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-390">u</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-391" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-392">V</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-393"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-394" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-395"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-396" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-397"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-398" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-399">N</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-400" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-401">V</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-402">0</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-403" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-404" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-405">1</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-406">/</span><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-407">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-7"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>σ</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>1</mn><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>φ</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi></mrow></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><msub><mrow><mi>σ</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>∞</mi></mrow></msub><mo>+</mo><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mn>3</mn><msqrt><mn>2</mn></msqrt><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><msub><mrow><mi>β</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">L</mi></mrow></msub><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mrow><mi>φ</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi></mrow></msub><mo>/</mo><msub><mrow><mi>r</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">A</mi></mrow></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><msqrt><mrow><mo>∈</mo><msub><mrow><mo>∈</mo></mrow><mrow><mn>0</mn></mrow></msub><mi>R</mi><mi>T</mi><mo>/</mo><msup><mrow><mi>V</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></msup></mrow></msqrt><msub><mrow><mi>u</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi></mrow></msub><msup><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><msub><mrow><mi>N</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">V</mi><mn>0</mn></mrow></msub><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn><mo>/</mo><mn>2</mn></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(6)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">Here, <i>φ</i><sub>A</sub> is the volume fraction of the inert phase A; σ<sub>∞</sub> is the conductance of the M<sup>+</sup>X<sup>–</sup> system; <i>β</i><sub>L</sub> takes into account the parallel switching correction; <i>r</i><sub>A</sub> is the mean radius of the A particles; <i>V</i><sup>m</sup> is the molar volume; <i>u</i><sub>V</sub> is the mobility of vacancies; and <i>N</i><sub>V</sub><sub>0</sub> is the vacancy concentration at the interface. The quantitative concentration profile <i>C</i><sub><i>v</i></sub>(<i>x</i>), Debye length λ, and conductivity enhancement <i>σ</i><sub>m</sub> can be determined if the relevant Gibb’s free energy, surface concentration, and mobilities are known. This has been done for the AgCl:Al<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> and AgCl:SiO<sub>2</sub> systems. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref40 ref41 ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41 ref42" aria-label="reference 40 to 42">(40−42)</a></div><div class="NLM_p">We suggest that similar nanoionics effects take place in our NaPF<sub>6</sub>-based electrolyte:Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrode system. We are, however, not able to provide a quantitative answer for the concentration profile, because the relevant Gibbs free energy, surface concentration, and mobilities are not known. We suggest that the capacity seen in our electrochemical cells and capacity enhancement with a nanostructured electrode is due to enhancement of Na<sup>+</sup> ion storage and conductivity at the interphase in the system based on the following: (1) Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is a ceramic with the highest known conductivity; DFT calculations indicate Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to be without a band gap at the Fermi level. (2) The carbon we used in the slurry is also conducting, so it seems that the slurry is an electrical conductor. (3) Our exhaustive experimental and theoretical studies have shown that Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> is not a Na<sup>+</sup> ion conductor. Following the nanoionics phenomenon, therefore, we hypothesize that a space charge region develop at the interfacial region between the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrode and the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-based electrolyte that becomes a storage and a conduit for Na<sup>+</sup> ion conduction (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e).</div><div class="NLM_p">Simple calculations (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>) based on close packing of 50 nm diameter bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and 8.5 nm diameter Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs show that the space charge layer length is inversely proportional to the square of the particle diameter, 1/<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>. The capacity further depends on the conductivity of the space charge layer, given above by <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq6" aria-label="eq 6">eq 6</a>. Many terms on the conductivity equation are sample-specific, and we do not have definite numerical values for our Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>/NaPF<sub>6</sub> system; thus, we attempt to make some simple assumptions. The first term in <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq6" aria-label="eq 6">eq 6</a> is the conductivity of pure NaPF<sub>6</sub>-based electrolyte, and it is the same for both bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>- and Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrodes. The value of <i>β</i><sub>L</sub> is used as a number close to unity for parallel switching, so we may assume it to be the same for both systems. Also, the volume fraction of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> (<i>φ</i><sub>A</sub>) is the same (70%) for both systems. The terms under the square root sign are the internal properties of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, and they are also the same for both systems. Finally, the mobility of vacancies (<i>u</i><sub>v</sub>) and vacancy concentration at the interface (<i>N</i><sub>V</sub><sub>0</sub>) refer to the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-based electrolyte, and it is reasonable to assume these values to remain the same when we change over from bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> to Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NPs. It seems that the only factor influencing the conductivity enhancement is the inverse of the particle radius, 1/<i>r</i>.</div><div class="NLM_p">Combining the effects of surface charge storage (1/<i>r</i>), nanoionics-induced space charge layer length (1/<i>r</i><sup>2</sup>), and the conductivity enhancements (1/<i>r</i>), we can qualitatively see that the capacity in the electrodes composed of NPs should be significantly larger than those made with bulk Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>. In practice, however, we see a capacity enhancement by a factor of only 2 for the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based system. A possible explanation for the discrepancy is that only the nanosizing effect cannot provide a comprehensive understanding of the nanostructuring phenomenon. Small nanoparticles have an overwhelming percentage of atoms on their surfaces with a number of dangling bonds, which result in detrimental effects─unwanted side reactions including electrolyte decomposition─on battery performance. We believe that such effects may lower the capacity of the cells with NP anodes.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The final point that needs to be addressed is that the 100 mA h/g capacity of the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrode still corresponds to on the order of 3736 Na<sup>+</sup> capacity per NP, as described earlier. We believe that the difference between our calculation (see <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>) and the limited storage ability of NP facets not being able to store more than one free charge and additional storage induced by nanoionics is related to a dynamic effect. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>e, this dynamic capacity could entail a capacitive effect between the Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the space charge region and the electrons in the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrode.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i24">Summary and Conclusions</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The concept of the SIB as a viable alternative to LIBs deserves serious effort because sodium is an element that is indeed in unlimited supply on the earth. At the same time, the concept of IS-TMO, especially Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, as a conversion-type electrode for SIB is worthy of investigation. Just like sodium, iron oxides are easily sourceable, are stable in the ambient, contribute to a minimal environmental footprint, and are easily recyclable. Our efforts, however, demonstrate that the widely accepted notion of using a knowledge base for LIBs in the construction of SIBs has limited reach. Specifically, a combination of electrochemical analysis and diagnostics, X-ray-based and spectroscopic analysis, and first principle modeling shows that Thackeray et al.’s model that has been used successfully to demonstrate the intercalation to conversion of Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> as anodes for LIBs is unworkable for SIBs. We instead propose a model of sodiation electrochemistry in Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> in terms of the creation of a space charge channel between the Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> NP-based electrode and the NaPF<sub>6</sub>-based electrolyte.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i25">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c13016?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.2c13016</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist"><li><p class="inline">Crystal structures, DoD and SoC sample descriptions, Li-ion half-cell description, d<i>Q</i>/d<i>V</i> plots, nanoparticle surface chemistry calculations, and EDS elemental mapping plots (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.2c13016/suppl_file/am2c13016_si_001.pdf">am2c13016_si_001.pdf (676.96 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.2c13016" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit3R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">3</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Materials Design for High-Safety Sodium-Ion Battery</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Yang, Chao; Xin, Sen; Mai, Liqiang; You, Ya</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Advanced Energy Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">11</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">2</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2000974</span>CODEN:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review.  Sodium-ion batteries, with their evident superiority in resource abundance and cost, are emerging as promising next-generation energy storage systems for large-scale applications, such as smart grids and low-speed elec. vehicles.  Accidents related to fires and explosions for batteries are a reminder that safety is prerequisite for energy storage systems, esp. when aiming for grid-scale use.  In a typical electrochem. secondary battery, the elec. power is stored and released via processes that generate thermal energy, leading to temp. increments in the battery system, which is the main cause for battery thermal abuse.  The investigation of the energy generated during the chem./electrochem. reactions is of paramount importance for battery safety, unfortunately, it has not received the attention it deserves.  In this review, the fundamentals of the heat generation, accumulation, and transportation in a battery system are summarized and recent key research on materials design to improve sodium-ion battery safety is highlighted.  Several effective materials design concepts are also discussed.  This review is designed to arouse the attention of researcher and scholars and inspire further improvements in battery safety.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqhWwPcmTnIjLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgb-44_4utO7Q"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXptlKqu7g%253D&amp;md5=965017c33a0909d33f1c526714c7ca3b</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref4'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1002/cssc.201801879"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xiao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Rojo, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, X.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Hard Carbon as Sodium-Ion Battery Anodes: Progress and Challenges</span>. <i>ChemSusChem</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2019</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>12</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">133</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">144</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review is given.  Hard C (HC) is the state-of-the-art anode material for Na-ion batteries due to its excellent overall performance, wide availability, and relatively low cost.  Recently, tremendous effort has been invested to elucidate the sodium storage mechanism in HC, and to explore synthetic approaches that can enhance the performance and lower the cost.  However, disagreements remain in the field, particularly on the fundamental questions of ion transfer and storage and the ideal HC structure for high performance.  This paper provides an anal. and summary of the theor. limitations of HC, discrepancies in the storage mechanism, and methods to improve the performance.  Future research on developing ideal structured HCs, advanced electrolytes, and optimized electrolyte-electrode interphases are proposed on the basis of recent progress.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpc09euZGW0wbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljPehqTeNuORw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXit1Oqu7jE&amp;md5=c493076faac0ea6afd3bc0c7a34a1810</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref5'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1021/nl3019559"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ha, D.-H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Islam, M.                         cas_cit5R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">5</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Binder-Free and Carbon-Free Nanoparticle Batteries: A Method for Nanoparticle Electrodes without Polymeric Binders or Carbon Black</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Ha, Don-Hyung; Islam, Mohammad A.; Robinson, Richard D.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nano Letters</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">In this work, we have developed a new fabrication method for nanoparticle (NP) assemblies for Li-ion battery electrodes that require no addnl. support or conductive materials such as polymeric binders or carbon black.  By eliminating these additives, we are able to improve the battery capacity/wt. ratio.  The NP film is formed by using electrophoretic deposition (EPD) of colloidally synthesized, monodisperse cobalt NPs that are transformed through the nanoscale Kirkendall effect into hollow Co3O4.  EPD forms a network of NPs that are mech. robust and elec. connected, enabling them to act as the Li-ion battery anode.  The morphol. change through cycles indicates stable 5-10 nm NPs form after the first lithiation remained throughout the cycling process.  This NP-film battery made without binders and conductive additives shows high gravimetric (&gt;830 mAh/g) and volumetric capacities (&gt;2100 mAh/cm3) even after 50 cycles.  Because similar films made from drop-casting do not perform well under equal conditions, EPD is seen as the crit. step to create good contacts between the particles and electrodes resulting in this significant improvement in battery electrode assembly.  This is a promising system for colloidal nanoparticles and a template for investigating the mechanism of lithiation and delithiation of NPs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrzwnt2u6Ml8rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljPehqTeNuORw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC38Xhtleisb7K&amp;md5=84d5493a5575d92615dcbeb97c429a38</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref6'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1088/1361-6528/aaa231"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Islam, M.                         cas_cit6R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">6</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High capacity lithium ion batteries composed of cobalt oxide nanoparticle anodes and Raman spectroscopic analysis of nanoparticle strain dynamics in batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Islam, Mohammad A.; Zuba, Mateusz; DeBiase, Vincent; Noviasky, Nicholas; Hawley, Christopher J.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanotechnology</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">IOP Publishing Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Cobalt nanoparticle thin films were electrophoretically deposited on copper current collectors and were annealed into thin films of hollow Co3O4 nanoparticles.  These thin films were directly used as the anodes of lithium ion batteries (LIBs) without the addn. of conducting carbons and bonding agents.  LIBs thus fabricated show high gravimetric capacities and long cycle lives.  For ≈1.0 μm thick Co3O4 nanoparticle films the gravimetric capacities of the batteries were more than 800 mAh g-1 at a current rate of C/15, which is about 90%of the theor. max.  Addnl., the batteries were able to undergo 200 charge/discharge cycles at a relatively fast rate of C/5 and maintain 50% of the initial capacity.  In order to understand the electrochem. of lithiation in the context of nanoparticles, Raman spectra were collected at different stages of the electrode cycles to det. the chem. and structural changes in the nanomaterials.  Our results indicate that initially the electrode nanoparticles were under significant strain and as the battery underwent many cycles of charging/discharging the nanoparticles experienced progressive strain relaxation.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGocNaTQa-j5wrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljPehqTeNuORw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXitFKhs7rN&amp;md5=add38d0c12acea7ba3c37a7508d6cad0</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref7"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref7'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">7</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit7" data-doi="10.1002/adma.201000717"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cabana, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Monconduit, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Larcher, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Palacín, M.         </div><div class="casAbstract">This review highlights the recent developments and the future prospects of the use of phases that react through conversion reactions as both cathode and anode materials in Li-ion batteries.  By moving beyond classical intercalation reactions, a variety of low cost compds. with gravimetric specific capacities that are 2-5 times larger than those attained with currently used materials, such as graphite and LiCoO2, can be achieved.                 10.1021/cm901452z</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D22%26publication_year%3D2010%26pages%3D587-603%26journal%3DChem.%2BMater.%26author%3DJ.%2BB.%2BGoodenough%26author%3DY.%2BKim%26title%3DChallenges%2Bfor%2BRechargeable%2BLi%2BBatteries%26doi%3D10.1021%252Fcm901452z&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c13016&amp;doiOfLink=10.1021%2Fcm901452z&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit8R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">8</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Challenges for Rechargeable Li Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Goodenough, John B.; Kim, Youngsik</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemistry of Materials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2010</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">22</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">3</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">587-603</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMATEX</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0897-4756</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">A review of challenges for further development of Li rechargeable batteries for elec. vehicles.  Most important is safety, which requires development of a nonflammable electrolyte with either a larger window between its LUMO and HOMO or a constituent (or additive) that can develop rapidly a solid/electrolyte interface (SEI) layer to prevent plating of Li on a carbon anode during a fast charge of the battery.  A high Li+-ion cond. (σLi &gt; 10-4 S/cm) in the electrolyte and across the electrode/electrolyte interface is needed for a power battery.  Important also is an increase in the d. of the stored energy, which is the product of the voltage and capacity of reversible Li insertion/extn. into/from the electrodes.  It will be difficult to design a better anode than carbon, but carbon requires formation of an SEI layer, which involves an irreversible capacity loss.  The design of a cathode composed of environmentally benign, low-cost materials that has its electrochem. potential μC well-matched to the HOMO of the electrolyte and allows access to two Li atoms per transition-metal cation would increase the energy d., but it is a daunting challenge.  Two redox couples can be accessed where the cation redox couples are pinned at the top of the O 2p bands, but to take advantage of this possibility, it must be realized in a framework structure that can accept more than one Li atom per transition-metal cation.  Moreover, such a situation represents an intrinsic voltage limit of the cathode, and matching this limit to the HOMO of the electrolyte requires the ability to tune the intrinsic voltage limit.  Finally, the chem. compatibility in the battery must allow a long service life.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoK49kJ9os4ALVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghv7jSQpLVeg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXhtVGktbfF&amp;md5=f902e4bc406fd0571064619bb4d37381</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref9"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref9'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">9</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit9" data-doi="10.1149/1.3254160"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Komaba, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Mikumo, T.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yabuuchi, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ogata, A.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yoshida, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yamada, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemical Insertion of Li and Na Ions into Nanocrystalline Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> and α–Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> for Rechargeable Batteries</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit9R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">9</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrochemical Insertion of Li and Na Ions into Nanocrystalline Fe3O4 and α-Fe2O3 for Rechargeable Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Komaba, Shinichi; Mikumo, Takashi; Yabuuchi, Naoaki; Ogata, Atsushi; Yoshida, Hiromi; Yamada, Yasuhiro</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of the Electrochemical Society</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">Fe3O4 powders with different particle sizes on av. (400, 100, and 10 nm) were prepd. and characterized by x-ray diffraction, transmission electron microscopy, Mossbauer spectroscopy, and electrochem. methods.  To examine the electrochem. activity of Fe3O4 in relation to the particle size effect, galvanostatic cycling tests in aprotic electrolytes contg. lithium or sodium ions were conducted.  The electrochem. activity was significantly enhanced as the mean particle size decreased.  The nanocrystd. Fe3O4 (10 nm) prepd. by pptn. method delivered 190 mA-h/g of the rechargeable capacity in the voltage range of 2-3 V in a lithium-ion contg. electrolyte, whereas the 400 and 100 nm Fe3O4 powders showed 10 and 80 mA-h/g of the rechargeable capacity, resp.  An ex situ x-ray diffraction study for the electrochem. cycled samples suggested the partly reversible Fe ion migration from the tetrahedral sites to the octahedral sites with a retained spinel framework structure.  The nanocrystd. Fe3O4 as well as α-Fe2O3 were highly electrochem. active in the sodium salt electrolyte.  The rechargeable capacity of 160 and 170 mA-h/g with excellent capacity retention was obtained for nanocryst. Fe3O4 and α-Fe2O3, resp.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGpwV7ECJe65drVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghv7jSQpLVeg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXhsFagurzP&amp;md5=83e3ad39a93b30ce302f88017fee325d</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref10"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref10'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">10</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit10" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2013.06.159"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Valvo, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lindgren, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lafont, U.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Björefors, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Edström, K.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Towards More Sustainable Negative Electrodes in Na-ion Batteries via Nanostructured Iron Oxide</span>. <i>J.                         cas_cit10R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">10</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Towards more sustainable negative electrodes in Na-ion batteries via nanostructured iron oxide</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Valvo, M.; Lindgren, F.; Lafont, U.; Bjoerefors, F.; Edstroem, K.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Power Sources</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2014</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Na-ion technol. could emerge as an alternative to Li-ion batteries due to limited costs and vast availability of sodium, as well as its similar chem.  Several Na-rich compds. were proposed as pos. electrodes, whereas suitable neg. counterparts were not found yet.  Nanostructured iron oxide is reported here for the 1st time as a potentially viable neg. electrode for Na-ion cells based on conventional electrolytes and composite coatings with CM-cellulose.  Electrochem. reactions of Na+ and Li+ ions with nanostructured Fe2O3 are analyzed and compared.  Initial sodiation of Fe2O3 yields a sloping profile in a voltage range characteristic for oxide conversion, which instead generates a typical plateau upon lithiation.  Application of such earth-abundant, nontoxic material in upcoming Na-ion batteries is potentially groundbreaking, since it offers important advantages, namely: i. simple and cost-effective synthesis of Fe2O3 nanostructures at low temps.; ii. cheaper and more sustainable cell fabrication with higher energy densities, e.g., use of natural, water-sol. binders, as well as Al for both current collectors; iii. electrochem. performances with specific gravimetric capacities exceeding 400 mAh g-1 at 40 mA g-1, accompanied by decent sp. vol. energy densities, e.g., ≈1.22 Wh cm-3, provided that cycle inefficiencies and long-term durability are addressed.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoMUA0bO_kCk7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghv7jSQpLVeg"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit11R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">11</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">A rationally designed dual role anode material for lithium-ion and sodium-ion batteries: case study of eco-friendly Fe3O4</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Hariharan, Srirama; Saravanan, Kuppan; Ramar, Vishwanathan; Balaya, Palani</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Physical Chemistry Chemical Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2013</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">15</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">8</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">2945-2953</span>CODEN:

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Identifying dual role electrode materials capable of storing both lithium and sodium are thought to be highly relevant, as these materials could find potential applications simultaneously in lithium- and sodium-ion batteries.  In this regard, the concept of dual alkali storage is demonstrated in Fe3O4 anode material undergoing conversion reaction.  To enable improved storage, a rational active material and electrode design is proposed.  Accordingly, the following features were simultaneously incorporated into the design: (a) an optimal particle size, (b) a conducting matrix, (c) adequately large active material surface area and (d) strong electrode material-current collector integrity.  Electrodes incorporating this rational design exhibit excellent high-rate performance and impressive cyclability during lithium storage.  For instance, Fe3O4 electrodes deliver a charge capacity of 950 mA-h/g at 1.2C (∼2.6 times higher than graphite and 5.4 times higher than Li4Ti5O12).  Further, these electrodes show no signs of capacity fade even up to 1100 cycles.  Impressively, the cells could also be charged-discharged to 65% of their theor. capacity in just 5 min or 12C (11.11 A/g).  The rate performance and cyclability of lithium storage achieved here are amongst the highest reported values in the literature for the conversion reaction in Fe3O4.  Besides lithium storage, the dual role of this anode is shown by demonstrating its sodium storage ability by conversion reaction.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGq1Ci_Jjiz4hrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lghv7jSQpLVeg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC3sXhvVChu70%253D&amp;md5=3227d1451a01a4a00d2e9d504da1dc9e</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref12"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref12'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">12</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit12" data-doi="10.1016/0025-5408(82)90029-0"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Thackeray, M. M.; David, W. I. F.; Goodenough, J. B.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Research Bulletin</span>

    </div><div class="casAbstract">Li was inserted into Fe3O4 and α-Fe2O3 at room temp. both chem. and electrochem.; a compositional range 0 &lt;x&lt;2 was established for both LixFe3O4 and LixFe2O3.  Powder x-ray diffraction data of Li1.5Fe3O4 indicate that the [Fe2]O4 subarray of the spinel structure remains intact; the A-site Fe3+ ions are displaced to empty octahedral positions and the Li+ ions in excess of x = 1 are located in tetrahedral sites.                         cas_cit14R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">14</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Visualizing non-equilibrium lithiation of spinel oxide via in situ transmission electron microscopy</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Kai; Zhang, Sen; Li, Jing; Yu, Xiqian; Meng, Qingping; Zhu, Yizhou; Hu, Enyuan; Sun, Ke; Yun, Hongseok; Yang, Xiao-Qing; Zhu, Yimei; Gan, Hong; Mo, Yifei; Stach, Eric A.; Murray, Christopher B.; Su, Dong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nature Communications</span>

    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">7</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Spinel transition metal oxides are important electrode materials for lithium-ion batteries, whose lithiation undergoes a two-step reaction, whereby intercalation and conversion occur in a sequential manner.  These two reactions are known to have distinct reaction dynamics, but it is unclear how their kinetics affects the overall electrochem. response.  Here we explore the lithiation of nanosized magnetite by employing a strain-sensitive, bright-field scanning transmission electron microscopy approach.  This method allows direct, real-time, high-resoln. visualization of how lithiation proceeds along specific reaction pathways.  We find that the initial intercalation process follows a two-phase reaction sequence, whereas further lithiation leads to the coexistence of three distinct phases within single nanoparticles, which has not been previously reported to the best of our knowledge.  We use phase-field theory to model and describe these non-equil. reaction pathways, and to directly correlate the obsd. phase evolution with the battery's discharge performance.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqOH7xfQoUSXbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh966iwH7MFmg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XnslWktbc%253D&amp;md5=cf4b6da61c7355faa50d09bb3590bb20</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref15"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref15'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">15</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit15" data-doi="10.1038/nmat1672"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Taberna, P.                         cas_cit15R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">15</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">High rate capabilities Fe3O4-based Cu nano-architectured electrodes for lithium-ion battery applications</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Taberna, P.         (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2006</span>),

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Nature Publishing Group</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Battery technologies suffer from kinetic problems linked to the solid-state diffusion of Li in intercalation electrodes, the cond. of the electrolyte in some cases, and the quality of interfaces.  For Li-ion technol. the latter effect is esp. acute when conversion rather than intercalation electrodes are used.  Nano-architectured electrodes have enhanced kinetics, but their realization is cumbersome.  To tackle this issue for the conversion electrode material Fe3O4, a 2-step electrode design was used - electrochem.-assisted template growth of Cu nanorods onto a current collector followed by electrochem. plating of Fe3O4.  With these electrodes a factor of 6 improvement in power d. over planar electrodes was obtained while maintaining the same total discharge time.  The capacity at the 8C rate was 80% of the total capacity and was sustained over 100 cycles.  The origin of the large hysteresis between charge and discharge, intrinsic to conversion reactions, is discussed and approaches to decrease it are proposed.  These findings promote the use of conversion reaction electrodes in future Li-ion batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGokbbD0KCK1L7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh966iwH7MFmg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD28XmsVGjsbk%253D&amp;md5=ae2ded43711ae60f4ae49685b4e6a554</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref16"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">16</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit16" data-doi="10.1149/1.3607983"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chevrier, V. The authors examine the viability of Na-ion anode materials based on Na alloys or hard carbons in terms of volumetric energy d.  Due to the bigger size of the Na atom compared to the Li atom, Na alloys would lead to anode materials with about half the volumetric energy d. of their Li analogs.  Volumetric energy densities obtainable with sodiated hard carbons would also be significantly less than those obtainable with lithiated graphite. Fe3O4@FeS combines the merits of FeS and Fe3O4 with high Na-storage capacity and superior cycling stability, resp.  The optimized Fe3O4@FeS electrode shows ultralong cycle life and outstanding rate capability.  For instance, it remains a capacity retention of 90.8% with a reversible capacity of 169 mAh g-1 after 750 cycles at 0.2 A g-1 and 151 mAh g-1 at a high c.d. of 2 A g-1, which is about 7.5 times in comparison to the Na-storage capacity of com. Fe3O4.  More importantly, the prepd. Fe3O4@FeS also exhibits excellent full-cell performance.  The assembled Fe3O4@FeS//Na3V2(PO4)2O2F sodium-ion full battery gives a reversible capacity of 157 mAh g-1 after 50 cycles at 0.5 A g-1 with a capacity retention of 92.3% and the Coulombic efficiency of around 100%, demonstrating its applicability for sodium-ion full batteries as a promising anode.  Furthermore, it is also disclosed that such superior electrochem. properties can be attributed to the pseudocapacitive behavior of FeS shell as demonstrated by the kinetics studies as well as the core-shell structure.  In view of the large-scale availability of com. precursor and ease of prepn., this study provide a scalable strategy to develop advanced anode materials for SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGrBb7Gs4YJuHrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lh966iwH7MFmg"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC1cXksVOltg%253D%253D&amp;md5=800f289306f9a89a1b2e0f9cecf93dea</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref19"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref19'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">19</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit19" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.6b09496"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jia, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhu, Q.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Cao, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Chen, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, B.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">3D-0D Graphene-Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span>. <i>ACS Appl.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">3D-0D Graphene-Fe3O4 Quantum Dot Hybrids as High-Performance Anode Materials for Sodium-Ion Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Liu, Huan; Jia, Mengqiu; Zhu, Qizhen; Cao, Bin; Chen, Renjie; Wang, Yu; Wu, Feng; Xu, Bin</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Applied Materials &amp; Interfaces</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">8</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">40</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">26878-26885</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">AAMICK</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Transition metal oxides can be considered as appealing candidates for sodium ion battery anode materials because these low-cost materials possess high capacity and enhanced safety.  However, the practical application of these materials is usually limited by their low electronic cond. and serious vol. change during the charging-discharging process.  Herein, we report the fabrication of 3D-0D graphene-Fe3O4 quantum dot hybrids by a facile one-pot hydrothermal approach as anode materials for sodium-ion batteries.  Fe3O4 quantum dots with an av. size of 4.9 nm are anchored on the surface of 3D structured graphene nanosheets homogeneously.  Such unique hierarchical structure are advantageous for enlarging the electrode/electrolyte interface area and enhancing the electrochem. activity of the hybrid materials, inhibiting particle aggregation of Fe3O4 and accommodating their vol. change during the charging-discharging process as well as enabling fast diffusion of electrons and rapid transfer of electrolyte ions.  Consequently, the 3D-0D graphene-Fe3O4 quantum dot hybrids exhibit ultrahigh sodium storage capacity (525 mAh/g at 30 mA/g), outstanding cycling stability (312 mAh/g after 200 cycles at 50 mA/g) and superior rate performance (56 mAh/g at 10 A/g).</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqySiZNElc5Z7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljrE3br0PzT2w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XhsFanu7rN&amp;md5=18e241446b270b8baeccc2c7fb9c0f0f</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref20"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">20</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit20" data-doi="10.1016/j.apsusc.2021.149163"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Miao, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wen, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yan, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lv, P.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sun, Q.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">In-situ plantation of Fe3O4@C nanoparticles on reduced graphene oxide nanosheet as high-performance anode for lithium/sodium-ion batteries</span>. <i>Appl.                         cas_cit21R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">21</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Plasma enabled Fe2O3/Fe3O4 nano-aggregates anchored on nitrogen-doped graphene as anode for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Wang, Qianqian; Ma, Yujie; Liu, Li; Yao, Shuyue; Wu, Wenjie; Wang, Zhongyue; Lv, Peng; Zheng, Jiajin; Yu, Kehan; Wei, Wei; Ostrikov, Kostya</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Nanomaterials</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2020</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">4</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">782</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">NANOKO</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">2079-4991</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">MDPI AG</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Low elec. cond. severely limits the application of Fe2O3 in lithium- and sodium-ion batteries.  In respect of this, we design and fabricate Fe2O3/Fe3O4 nano-aggregates anchored on nitrogen-doped graphene as an anode for sodium-ion batteries with the assistance of microwave plasma.  The highly conductive Fe3O4 in the composite can function as a highway of electron transport, and the voids and phase boundaries in the Fe2O3/Fe3O4 heterostructure facilitate Na+ ion diffusion into the nano-aggregates.  Furthermore, the Fe-O-C bonds between the nano-aggregates and graphene not only stabilize the structural integrity, but also enhance the charge transfer.  Consequently, the Fe2O3/Fe3O4/NG anode exhibits specific capacity up to 362 mAh g-1 at 100 mA g-1, excellent rate capability, and stable long-term cycling performance.  This multi-component-based heterostructure design can be used in anode materials for lithium- and sodium-ion batteries, and potential opens a new path for energy storage electrodes.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoDAaeGIPfmvbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljrE3br0PzT2w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXhtVGjsbzM&amp;md5=194e0a53680894a34b3fe809f559aee5</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref22"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">22</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit22" data-doi="10.1016/j.jcis.2020.01.018"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tao, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lv, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xu, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jiang, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Meng, Y.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Multi-heteroatom-doped dual carbon-confined Fe<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub> nanospheres as high-capacity and long-life anode materials for lithium/sodium ion batteries</span>. <i>J.                 10.1016/j.cej.2017.09.088</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D332%26publication_year%3D2018%26pages%3D260-266%26journal%3DChem.%2BEng.%2BJ.%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DW.%2BQin%26author%3DJ.%2BXie%26author%3DR.%2BLin%26author%3DZ.%2BWang%26author%3DL.%2BPan%26author%3DW.%2BMai%26title%3DRational%2Bdesign%2Bof%2BMoS2-reduced%2Bgraphene%2Boxide%2Bsponges%2Bas%2Bfree-standing%2Banodes%2Bfor%2Bsodium-ion%2Bbatteries%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.cej.2017.09.088&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c13016&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.cej.2017.09.088&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit23R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">23</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Rational design of MoS2-reduced graphene oxide sponges as free-standing anodes for sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Jinliang; Qin, Wei; Xie, Junpeng; Lin, Rui; Wang, Zilong; Pan, Likun; Mai, Wenjie</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2018</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">332</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">260-266</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Currently, the search for high capacity, low cost and free-standing electrodes for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) is one of the major challenges in energy storage field.  In this work, we rationally design MoS2-reduced graphene oxide (MS-RGO) sponges via a simple freeze-drying of ammonium tetrathiomolybdate-graphene oxide mixed soln. and a subsequent thermal treatment in N2/H2 atmosphere, and employ these sponges as free-standing anodes for SIBs.  The MS-RGO sponges exhibit a porous conducive structure that can facilitate the charge transport and thus show an excellent electrochem. performance.  The free-standing sponge electrodes display a maximal reversible specific capacity of 372.0 mAh g-1 (0.49 mAh cm-2) at a c.d. of 100 mA g-1 after 50 cycles.  Even at a high c.d. of 1 A g-1, a capacity of 192.2 mAh g-1 (0.25 mAh cm-2) is maintained after 345 cycles.  The results show that MS-RGO sponges are promising free-standing electrode materials for rechargeable SIBs.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqLjHLe0_L9DbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljrE3br0PzT2w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2sXhsFCisLbO&amp;md5=4971e0b77970b22a4b93ce013c871faf</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref24"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref24'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">24</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit24" data-doi="10.1016/j.cej.2020.127199"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Synergistic coupling of NiS<sub>1.03</sub> nanoparticle with S-doped reduced graphene oxide for enhanced lithium and sodium storage</span>. <i>Chem.                 10.1016/j.cej.2020.127199</span> </span><div class=" links-group getFTR"><div class="getFTR__content getFTR__placeholder"></div><span class="references__suffix"><a class="google-scholar" href="/action/getFTRLinkout?url=http%3A%2F%2Fscholar.google.com%2Fscholar_lookup%3Fhl%3Den%26volume%3D407%26publication_year%3D2021%26pages%3D127199%26journal%3DChem.%2BEng.%2BJ.%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DZ.%2BDing%26author%3DJ.%2BLi%26author%3DC.%2BWang%26author%3DL.%2BPan%26author%3DG.%2BWang%26title%3DSynergistic%2Bcoupling%2Bof%2BNiS1.03%2Bnanoparticle%2Bwith%2BS-doped%2Breduced%2Bgraphene%2Boxide%2Bfor%2Benhanced%2Blithium%2Band%2Bsodium%2Bstorage%26doi%3D10.1016%252Fj.cej.2020.127199&amp;doi=10.1021%2Facsami.2c13016&amp;doiOfLink=10.1016%2Fj.cej.2020.127199&amp;linkType=gs&amp;linkLocation=Reference&amp;linkSource=FULL_TEXT" target="_blank">Google Scholar</a></span><div class="casRecord" id="
                        cas_cit24R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">24</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Synergistic coupling of NiS1.03 nanoparticle with S-doped reduced graphene oxide for enhanced lithium and sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Jiabao; Ding, Zibiao; Li, Jinliang; Wang, Chengyin; Pan, Likun; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">407</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">127199</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">The interest on developing nickel sulfide based dual-role anode materials for both lithium-ion battery (LIBs) and sodium-ion battery (SIBs) has aroused broad attention.  However, increasing their lithium and sodium storage performances necessitates smart structure design and fabrication.  Herein, we demonstrate a facile strategy for the rational combination of NiS1.03 nanoparticles with sulfur doped reduced graphene oxide (NiS1.03/S-rGO), with the designed structure of ultrafine NiS1.03 nanoparticles uniformly anchored on or even wrapped in S-rGO.  The systematic electrochem. studies demonstrate that the NiS1.03/S-rGO exhibits high reversible capacities of 996.1 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1 and 337.8 mAh g-1 up to 2000 cycles at 4 A g-1 as an anode material for LIBs.  As for SIBs, the NiS1.03/S-rGO also displays high reversible capacities of 345.6 mAh g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 after 100 cycles and 242.1 mAh g-1 at 0.5 A g-1 after 200 cycles.  Addnl., detailed structure anal. illustrates that the superior electrochem. performances mainly originate from the robust composite architecture where the NiS1.03 nanoparticles and S-rGO are tightly bridged via the doped sulfur atoms at the hetero-interface through a synergistic coupling effect, simultaneously guaranteeing the electrode integrity and fast diffusion kinetics.  More importantly, the combination approach and mechanism understanding at the mol. level presented in this study show good promise on transition oxides/sulfides for high-performance alkali metal ion battery.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoHvqcDOlCQlLVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljrE3br0PzT2w"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3cXitVClsLfE&amp;md5=c888c948f082d98f67b89cc753e4c813</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref25"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref25'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">25</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit25" data-doi="10.1016/j.carbon.2020.10.092"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ding, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Pan, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Li, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, C.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, G.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Facile self-templating synthesis of layered carbon with N, S dual doping for highly efficient sodium storage</span>. <i>Carbon</i>  <span class="NLM_year" style="font-weight: bold;">2021</span>,  <span class="NLM_volume"><i>173</i></span>,  <span class="NLM_fpage">31</span>– <span class="NLM_lpage">40</span>, <span class="refDoi">&nbsp;DOI:

                        cas_cit25R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">25</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Facile self-templating synthesis of layered carbon with N, S dual doping for highly efficient sodium storage</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Li, Jiabao; Ding, Zibiao; Pan, Likun; Li, Jinliang; Wang, Chengyin; Wang, Guoxiu</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Carbon</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">173</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">31-40</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CRBNAH</span>;

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Nitrogen and sulfur co-doping is emerging as a promising approach to prominently improve the sodium storage performance of carbonaceous materials.  However, poisonous chems. and rigorous conditions are normally involved in their prepn., esp. in the sulfidation process.  Therefore, developing novel, facile and eco-friendly approaches to construct nitrogen and sulfur co-doped carbon (NSC) is still challenging.  In this work, a facile self-templating synthesis method is reported to allow the simple and green prepn. of NSC.  Particularly, small-mol. dithiooxamide can be directly converted to NSC through a facile thermal condensation process, serving as precursor, template, and self-doping agent simultaneously.  In account of its high ionic and electronic cond., increased interlayer spacing, unique layers assembled structure and efficient N, S co-doping, the NSC obtained at 800° exhibits excellent sodium storage performances, including prominent reversible capacity (316.1 mAh g-1 at 0.1 A g-1 ≤ 100 cycles), remarkable rate capability (178.3 mAh g-1 at 3 A g-1), and robust stability upon long-term cycling (213.6 mAh g-1 at 1 A g-1 up to 2000 cycles), rendering itself to be a promising candidate for practical sodium-ion battery.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoC-_YzCMJL3rVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0ljrE3br0PzT2w"> &gt;&gt;                         cas_cit26R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">26</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Insights into the storage mechanism of 3D nanoflower-like V3S4 anode in sodium-ion batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Zhang, Yajuan; Li, Jinliang; Ma, Liang; Li, Haibo; Xu, Xingtao; Liu, Xinjuan; Lu, Ting; Pan, Likun</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Chemical Engineering Journal (Amsterdam, Netherlands)</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2022</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">427</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue"></span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">130936</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">CMEJAJ</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1385-8947</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier B.V.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Over the past years, transition metal sulfides, with remarkable redox reversibility, favorable elec. cond. and superb theor. capacity, have received considerable attention on the development of electrode materials that are capable of efficient and reversible storage of sodium ions.  In this work, three-dimensional (3D) nanoflower-like V3S4 was applied into sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) as anode, which was successfully synthesized via a facile solvothermal process with post-calcination treatment.  The electrochem. measurements demonstrate that the as-prepd. V3S4 delivers a high reversible capacity of 499 mAh g-1 after 100 cycles at 0.1 A g-1.  Furthermore, even at a high c.d. of 5 A g-1, the V3S4 also exhibits an outstanding specific capacity of 299 mAh g-1 and a capacity retention rate of 58% compared with the capacity at 0.1 A g-1, which are superior to those of most vanadium sulfides for SIBs reported previously.  Remarkably, the ultrahigh sodium-ion storage performance of V3S4 should be ascribed to the unique nanoflower-like structure assembled by ultrathin nanosheets, which not only provides a large sp. surface area, but also offers an adequate cushion to suppress the vol. expansion during the sodiation-desodiation processes.  More importantly, operando Raman spectra, ex-situ X-ray diffraction and ex-situ XPS were employed to demonstrate the evolution of the structure and chem. valence of V3S4, revealing that the high capacity of V3S4 results from the cooperation of Na+ intercalation and conversion reaction.  The findings of this study provide a better understanding on the sodium storage mechanism of V3S4.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGoseqVtdgVzMrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhRPgGqRQAt_g"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BB3MXhsVagtrjK&amp;md5=f7a9ad6752588f4c0e48a01d79b210a6</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref27"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref27'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">27</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit27" data-doi="10.1016/S0022-4596(03)00294-9"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Shebanova, O.         </div><div class="casAbstract">A detailed Raman study on natural magnetite was carried out.  Raman spectra show four out of the five predicted Raman bands located at 668, 538, 306, and 193 rcm-1.  The location of the 5th, unobserved phonon mode, is inferred from spectra of other ferrites at 450-490 rcm-1.  Polarized expts. on an oriented single crystal provide a new interpretation of the Raman spectrum with the following assignment for symmetries of the obsd. modes: A1g for 668 rcm-1, Eg for 306 rcm-1, and T2g for 538, 193, and 450-490 rcm-1.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Mono- (Li/Na), di- (Mg/Ca) and trivalent (Al) ion intercalation is investigated in the α and ζ phases of V2O5.  D. functional theory is used to calc. the phys. and electronic properties of each in the context of secondary battery cathodes.  Various exchange-correlation functionals and van der Waals correction schemes are employed in calcg. lattice parameters, bandgaps, d. of states, cell voltages, and ion diffusion barriers.  For intercalations of similar charge transfer (i.e., Li0.67, Na0.67, Mg0.33, Ca0.33, and Al0.20), Ca provides the highest voltage (2.95 V) in α-V2O5 with both PBE and PBE-D3. Monovalent ion diffusion barriers are calcd. and show that Li and Na possess barriers of 0.39/0.32 eV and 1.27/1.09 eV in α/ζ-V2O5, resp.  A full voltage profile for Na intercalation in each phase detd. the initial voltage in ζ-V2O5 (4.00 V) is higher than that in α-V2O5 (3.4 V), but lacks in specific capacity (168 mAhg-1 vs. 235 mAhg-1).         </div><div class="casAbstract">The diffusion of ions in solid materials plays an important role in many aspects of materials science such as the geol. evolution of minerals, materials synthesis, and in device performance across several technologies.  For example, the realization of multivalent (MV) batteries, which offer a realistic route to superseding the electrochem. performance of Li-ion batteries, hinges on the discovery of host materials that possess adequate mobility of the MV intercalant to support reasonable charge and discharge times.  This has proven esp. challenging, motivating the current investigation of ion mobility (Li+, Mg2+, Zn2+, Ca2+, and Al3+) in spinel Mn2O4, olivine FePO4, layered NiO2, and orthorhombic δ-V2O5.  In this study, we not only quant. assess these structures as candidate cathode materials, but also isolate the chem. and structural descriptors that govern MV diffusion.  Our finding that matching the intercalant site preference to the diffusion path topol. of the host structure controls mobility more than any other factor leads to practical and implementable guidelines to find fast-diffusing MV ion conductors.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqgXJVHD4YBq7Vg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgjAPbgjWesOw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC2MXht12ksrjL&amp;md5=68610753f0a38d3627470279a0b54c19</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref36"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref36'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">36</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit36" data-doi="10.1088/0953-8984/21/28/285301"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Islam, M.                         cas_cit36R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">36</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Electrostatic properties of maghemite (γ-Fe2O3) nanocrystalline quantum dots determined by electrophoretic deposition</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Islam, Mohammad A.; Xia, Shengguo</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Journal of Physics: Condensed Matter</span>

        </div><div class="casAbstract">We applied a d.c. elec. field between 2 flat electrodes to attract thermally charged maghemite (γ-Fe2O3) nanocryst. quantum dots dissolved in hexane to form smooth, robust, large area and apparently identical films of equal thickness on both electrodes.  Visible microscopy, SEM, at. force microscopy and profilometry showed that the electrophoretically deposited dot films were very smooth with an root-mean-square roughness of ∼10 nm for ∼0.2 μm thick films.  The films were of high quality.  They did not re-dissolve in hexane (as do those formed by dry casting), which is a good solvent for these dots, or in common cleaning solvents such as H2O, alcs. and acetone.  The deposition on both electrodes implies there are both pos. and neg. thermally charged dots, unlike conventional electrophoretic deposition.  We used simple thermodn. to explain the results of electrophoretic deposition macroscopically.  To connect the macroscopic nature of the deposition to the microscopic nature of the dots we performed electrophoretic mobility measurements of the dots and the results seem to complement the thermodn. treatment.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGqI_5QtoHhBnbVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lgjAPbgjWesOw"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BD1MXhtVWnsb3L&amp;md5=5c171d08a30bab1a42f4aa0c9b21094a</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref37"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref37'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">37</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit37" data-doi="10.1016/0022-3697(85)90172-6"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Maier, J.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Space Charge Regions in Solid Two-phase Systems and Their Conduction Contribution─I.     </div><div class="casAbstract">The space charge contribution to the conductance is calcd. for 2-phase systems (bicrystal and dispersion) where the 2nd phase is a chem. inert phase such as alumina and silica.  A great effect is to be expected if the 2nd phase is surface reactive so that the mobile ion is stabilized or destabilized at the interface.  As a consequence, mobile ions are sucked out of the vol. or driven into it, resulting in an enhancement of the concn. of vacancies or interstitials, resp.  The derived equations, which describe the problem quant., agree very well with exptl. results in the AgCl:Al2O3 and AgCl:SiO2 systems and are applied to literature examples.
